    Peasants' Revolt
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">

     <p>
      The
      <b>
       Peasants' Revolt
      </b>
      , also called
      <b>
       Wat Tyler's Rebellion
      </b>
      or the
      <b>
       Great Rising
      </b>
      , was a major uprising across large parts of England in 1381. The revolt had various causes, including the socio-economic and political tensions generated by the
      <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">
       Black Death
      </a>
      in the 1340s, the high taxes resulting from the conflict with France during the
      <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years' War">
       Hundred Years' War
      </a>
      , and instability within the local leadership of London. The final trigger for the revolt was the intervention of a royal official, John Bampton, in
      <a href="/wiki/Essex" title="Essex">
       Essex
      </a>
      on 30 May 1381. His attempts to collect unpaid
      <a href="/wiki/Tax_per_head" title="Tax per head">
       poll taxes
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Brentwood,_Essex" title="Brentwood, Essex">
       Brentwood
      </a>
      ended in a violent confrontation, which rapidly spread across the south-east of the country. A wide spectrum of rural society, including many local artisans and village officials, rose up in protest, burning court records and opening the local gaols. The rebels sought a reduction in taxation, an end to the system of
      <a href="/wiki/Unfree_labour" title="Unfree labour">
       unfree labour
      </a>
      known as
      <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">
       serfdom
      </a>
      and the removal of the King's senior officials and law courts.
     </p>
     <p>
      Inspired by the sermons of the radical cleric
      <a href="/wiki/John_Ball_(priest)" title="John Ball (priest)">
       John Ball
      </a>
      , and led by
      <a href="/wiki/Wat_Tyler" title="Wat Tyler">
       Wat Tyler
      </a>
      , a contingent of
      <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">
       Kentish
      </a>
      rebels advanced on London. They were met at
      <a href="/wiki/Blackheath,_London" title="Blackheath, London">
       Blackheath
      </a>
      by representatives of the royal government, who unsuccessfully attempted to persuade them to return home. King
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_II_of_England" title="Richard II of England">
       Richard II
      </a>
      , then aged 14, retreated to the safety of the
      <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">
       Tower of London
      </a>
      , but most of the royal forces were abroad or in northern England. On 13 June, the rebels entered London and, joined by many local townsfolk, attacked the gaols, destroyed the
      <a href="/wiki/Savoy_Palace" title="Savoy Palace">
       Savoy Palace
      </a>
      , set fire to law books and buildings in
      <a href="/wiki/Temple,_London" title="Temple, London">
       the Temple
      </a>
      , and killed anyone associated with the royal government. The following day, Richard met the rebels at
      <a href="/wiki/Mile_End" title="Mile End">
       Mile End
      </a>
      and acceded to most of their demands, including the abolition of serfdom. Meanwhile, rebels entered the Tower of London, killing the
      <a href="/wiki/Simon_Sudbury" title="Simon Sudbury">
       Lord Chancellor
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hales" title="Robert Hales">
       Lord High Treasurer
      </a>
      , whom they found inside.
     </p>
     <p>
      On 15 June, Richard left the city to meet with Tyler and the rebels at
      <a href="/wiki/Smithfield,_London" title="Smithfield, London">
       Smithfield
      </a>
      . Violence broke out, and Richard's party killed Tyler. Richard defused the tense situation long enough for London's mayor,
      <a href="/wiki/William_Walworth" title="William Walworth">
       William Walworth
      </a>
      , to gather a militia from the city and disperse the rebel forces. Richard immediately began to re-establish order in London and rescinded his previous grants to the rebels. The revolt had also spread into
      <a href="/wiki/East_Anglia" title="East Anglia">
       East Anglia
      </a>
      , where the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">
       University of Cambridge
      </a>
      was attacked and many royal officials were killed. Unrest continued until the intervention of
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_le_Despenser" title="Henry le Despenser">
       Henry le Despenser
      </a>
      , who defeated a rebel army at the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_North_Walsham" title="Battle of North Walsham">
       Battle of North Walsham
      </a>
      on 25 or 26 June. Troubles extended north to
      <a href="/wiki/York" title="York">
       York
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Beverley" title="Beverley">
       Beverley
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Scarborough,_North_Yorkshire" title="Scarborough, North Yorkshire">
       Scarborough
      </a>
      , and as far west as
      <a href="/wiki/Bridgwater" title="Bridgwater">
       Bridgwater
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Somerset" title="Somerset">
       Somerset
      </a>
      . Richard mobilised 4,000 soldiers to restore order. Most of the rebel leaders were tracked down and executed; by November, at least 1,500 rebels had been killed.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Peasants' Revolt has been widely studied by academics. Late 19th-century historians used a range of sources from contemporary
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chroniclers" title="Chroniclers">
       chroniclers
      </a>
      to assemble an account of the uprising, and these were supplemented in the 20th century by research using court records and local archives. Interpretations of the revolt have shifted over the years. It was once seen as a defining moment in English history, but modern academics are less certain of its impact on subsequent social and economic history. The revolt heavily influenced the course of the Hundred Years' War, by deterring later Parliaments from raising additional taxes to pay for military campaigns in France. The revolt has been widely used in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socialist" title="Socialist">
       socialist
      </a>
      literature, including by the author
      <a href="/wiki/William_Morris" title="William Morris">
       William Morris
      </a>
      , and remains a potent political symbol for the
      <a href="/wiki/Left-wing_politics" title="Left-wing politics">
       political left
      </a>
      , informing the arguments surrounding the introduction of the
      <a href="/wiki/Community_Charge" title="Community Charge">
       Community Charge
      </a>
      in the United Kingdom during the 1980s.
     </p>

        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19">
         <a href="#Legacy">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           4
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           Legacy
          </span>
         </a>
         <ul>
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20">
           <a href="#Historiography">
            <span class="tocnumber">
             4.1
            </span>
            <span class="toctext">
             Historiography
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21">
           <a href="#Popular_culture">
            <span class="tocnumber">
             4.2
            </span>
            <span class="toctext">
             Popular culture
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22">
         <a href="#See_also">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           5
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           See also
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23">
         <a href="#Notes">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           6
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">

          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24">
         <a href="#References">
          <span class="tocnumber">

          </span>

        </li>

      </div>
     </div>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Background_and_causes">
       Background and causes
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background and causes">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">
       Economics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Economics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Consequences_of_the_Black_Death" title="Consequences of the Black Death">
       Consequences of the Black Death
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Peasants' Revolt was fed by the economic and social upheaval of the 14th century.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      At the start of the century, the majority of English people worked in the countryside, as part of a sophisticated economy that fed the country's towns and cities and supported an extensive international trade.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Across much of England, production was organised around
      <a href="/wiki/Manor" title="Manor">
       manors
      </a>
      , controlled by local lords including the
      <a href="/wiki/Gentry" title="Gentry">
       gentry
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_England" title="Religion in England">
       Church
      </a>
       and governed through a system of
      <a href="/wiki/Manorial_court" title="Manorial court">
       manorial courts
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some of the population were unfree
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serf" title="Serf">
       serfs
      </a>
      , who had to work on their lords' lands for a period each year, although the balance of free and unfree varied across England, and in the south-east there were relatively few serfs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some serfs were born unfree and could not leave their manors to work elsewhere without the consent of the local lord; others accepted limitations on their freedom as part of the tenure agreement for their farmland.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Population growth led to pressure on the available agricultural land, increasing the power of local landowners.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1348 a plague known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">
       Black Death
      </a>
      crossed from mainland Europe into England, rapidly killing an estimated 50 per cent of the population.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After an initial period of economic shock, England began to adapt to the changed economic situation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The death rate among the peasantry meant that suddenly land was relatively plentiful and manpower in much shorter supply.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Labourers could charge more for their work and, in the consequent competition for labour, wages were driven sharply upwards.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In turn, the profits of landowners were eroded.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The trading, commercial and financial networks in the towns disintegrated.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The authorities responded to the chaos with emergency legislation; the
      <a href="/wiki/Ordinance_of_Labourers_1349" title="Ordinance of Labourers 1349">
       Ordinance of Labourers
      </a>
      was passed in 1349, and the
      <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_Labourers_1351" title="Statute of Labourers 1351">
       Statute of Labourers
      </a>
      in 1351.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These attempted to fix wages at pre-plague levels, making it a crime to refuse work or to break an existing contract, imposing fines on those who transgressed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The system was initially enforced through special
      <a href="/wiki/Judicial_officer" title="Judicial officer">
       Justices
      </a>
      of Labourers and then, from the 1360s onwards, through the normal
      <a href="/wiki/Justice_of_the_peace" title="Justice of the peace">
       Justices of the Peace
      </a>
      , typically members of the local gentry.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although in theory these laws applied to both labourers seeking higher wages and to employers tempted to outbid their competitors for workers, they were in practice applied only to labourers, and then in a rather arbitrary fashion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The legislation was strengthened in 1361, with the penalties increased to include
      <a href="/wiki/Human_branding" title="Human branding">
       branding
      </a>
      and imprisonment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The royal government had not intervened in this way before, nor allied itself with the local landowners in quite such an obvious or unpopular way.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dyer_2009_285_18-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dyer_2009_285-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Over the next few decades, economic opportunities increased for the English peasantry.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some labourers took up specialist jobs that would have previously been barred to them, and others moved from employer to employer, or became servants in richer households.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These changes were keenly felt across the south-east of England, where the London market created a wide range of opportunities for farmers and artisans.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Local lords had the right to prevent serfs from leaving their manors, but when serfs found themselves blocked in the manorial courts, many simply left to work illegally on manors elsewhere.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Wages continued to rise, and between the 1340s and the 1380s the purchasing power of rural labourers increased by around 40 percent.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As the wealth of the lower classes increased, Parliament brought in
      <a href="/wiki/Statute_Concerning_Diet_and_Apparel_1363" title="Statute Concerning Diet and Apparel 1363">
       fresh laws in 1363
      </a>
      to prevent them from consuming expensive goods formerly only affordable by the elite. These
      <a href="/wiki/Sumptuary_law" title="Sumptuary law">
       sumptuary laws
      </a>
      proved unenforceable, but the wider labour laws continued to be firmly applied.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="War_and_finance">
       War and finance
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: War and finance">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Another factor in the revolt of 1381 was the conduct of the war with France. In 1337
      <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">
       Edward III of England
      </a>
      had pressed
      <a href="/wiki/English_claims_to_the_French_throne" title="English claims to the French throne">
       his claims to the French throne
      </a>
      , beginning a long-running conflict that became known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years' War">
       Hundred Years' War
      </a>
      . Edward had initial successes, but his campaigns were not decisive.
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_V_of_France" title="Charles V of France">
       Charles V of France
      </a>
      became more active in the conflict after 1369, taking advantage of his country's greater economic strength to commence cross-Channel raids on England.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By the 1370s, England's armies on the continent were under huge military and financial pressure; the garrisons in
      <a href="/wiki/Calais" title="Calais">
       Calais
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Brest,_France" title="Brest, France">
       Brest
      </a>
      alone, for example, were costing 36,000 a year to maintain, while military expeditions could consume 50,000 in only six months.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Edward died in 1377, leaving the throne to his grandson,
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_II_of_England" title="Richard II of England">
       Richard II
      </a>
      , then only ten years old.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Troupe_anglaise_d%C3%A9barquant_en_Normandie_XIVeme_siecle.jpg">
        <img alt="Medieval painting" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1395" data-file-width="1348" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Troupe_anglaise_d%C3%A9barquant_en_Normandie_XIVeme_siecle.jpg/220px-Troupe_anglaise_d%C3%A9barquant_en_Normandie_XIVeme_siecle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Troupe_anglaise_d%C3%A9barquant_en_Normandie_XIVeme_siecle.jpg/330px-Troupe_anglaise_d%C3%A9barquant_en_Normandie_XIVeme_siecle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Troupe_anglaise_d%C3%A9barquant_en_Normandie_XIVeme_siecle.jpg/440px-Troupe_anglaise_d%C3%A9barquant_en_Normandie_XIVeme_siecle.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Troupe_anglaise_d%C3%A9barquant_en_Normandie_XIVeme_siecle.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        English soldiers landing in
        <a href="/wiki/Normandy" title="Normandy">
         Normandy
        </a>
        , c. 13801400, during the
        <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years' War">
         Hundred Years' War
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Richard's government was formed around his uncles, most prominently the rich and powerful
      <a href="/wiki/John_of_Gaunt" title="John of Gaunt">
       John of Gaunt
      </a>
      , and many of his grandfather's former senior officials. They faced the challenge of financially sustaining the war in France. Taxes in the 14th century were raised on an
      <i>
       ad hoc
      </i>
      basis through Parliament, then comprising the
      <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">
       Lords
      </a>
      , the titled aristocracy and clergy; and the
      <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_England" title="House of Commons of England">
       Commons
      </a>
      , the representatives of the knights, merchants and senior gentry from across England.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These taxes were typically imposed on a household's movable possessions, such as their goods or stock.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The raising of these taxes affected the members of the Commons much more than the Lords.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      To complicate matters, the official statistics used to administer the taxes pre-dated the Black Death and, since the size and wealth of local communities had changed greatly since the plague, effective collection had become increasingly difficult.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Just before Edward's death, Parliament introduced a new form of taxation called the poll tax, which was levied at the rate of four pence on every person over the age of 14, with a deduction for married couples.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones2010Dunn201P51_34-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jones2010Dunn201P51-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Designed to spread the cost of the war over a broader economic base than previous tax levies, this round of taxation proved extremely unpopular but raised 22,000.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones2010Dunn201P51_34-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jones2010Dunn201P51-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The war continued to go badly and, despite raising some money through forced loans, the Crown returned to Parliament in 1379 to request further funds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Commons were supportive of the young King, but had concerns about the amounts of money being sought and the way this was being spent by the King's counsellors, whom they suspected of corruption.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A second poll tax was approved, this time with a sliding scale of taxes against seven different classes of English society, with the upper classes paying more in absolute terms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Widespread evasion proved to be a problem, and the tax only raised 18,600 far short of the 50,000 that had been hoped for.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In November 1380, Parliament was called together again in
      <a href="/wiki/Northampton" title="Northampton">
       Northampton
      </a>
      . Archbishop
      <a href="/wiki/Simon_Sudbury" title="Simon Sudbury">
       Simon Sudbury
      </a>
      , the new
      <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor" title="Lord Chancellor">
       Lord Chancellor
      </a>
      , updated the Commons on the worsening situation in France, a collapse in international trade, and the risk of the Crown having to default on its debts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Commons were told that the colossal sum of 160,000 was now required in new taxes, and arguments ensued between the royal council and Parliament about what to do next.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Parliament passed a third poll tax (this time on a flat-rate basis of 12 pence on each person over 15, with no allowance made for married couples) which they estimated would raise 66,666.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The third poll tax was highly unpopular and many in the south-east evaded it by refusing to register.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The royal council appointed new commissioners in March 1381 to interrogate local village and town officials in an attempt to find those who were refusing to comply.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The extraordinary powers and interference of these teams of investigators in local communities, primarily in the south-east and east of England, raised still further the tensions surrounding the taxes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Protest_and_authority">
       Protest and authority
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Protest and authority">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sheep_pen_(Luttrell_Psalter).png">
        <img alt="Medieval painting" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="664" data-file-width="1239" height="118" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Sheep_pen_%28Luttrell_Psalter%29.png/220px-Sheep_pen_%28Luttrell_Psalter%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Sheep_pen_%28Luttrell_Psalter%29.png/330px-Sheep_pen_%28Luttrell_Psalter%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Sheep_pen_%28Luttrell_Psalter%29.png/440px-Sheep_pen_%28Luttrell_Psalter%29.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sheep_pen_(Luttrell_Psalter).png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Sheep farming, from the
        <a href="/wiki/Luttrell_Psalter" title="Luttrell Psalter">
         Luttrell Psalter
        </a>
        , c. 132040
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The decades running up to 1381 were a rebellious, troubled period.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      London was a particular locus of unrest, and the activities of the city's politically active
      <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">
       guilds
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Fraternity" title="Fraternity">
       fraternities
      </a>
      often alarmed the authorities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Londoners resented the expansion of the royal legal system in the capital, in particular the increased role of the
      <a href="/wiki/Marshalsea_Court" title="Marshalsea Court">
       Marshalsea Court
      </a>
      in Southwark, which had begun to compete with the city authorities for judicial power in London.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The city's population also resented the presence of foreigners,
      <a href="/wiki/Flemish_people" title="Flemish people">
       Flemish
      </a>
      weavers in particular.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Londoners detested John of Gaunt because he was a supporter of the religious reformer
      <a href="/wiki/John_Wycliffe" title="John Wycliffe">
       John Wycliffe
      </a>
      , whom the London public regarded as a heretic.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      John of Gaunt was also engaged in a feud with the London elite and was rumoured to be planning to replace the elected
      <a href="/wiki/Lord_Mayor_of_London" title="Lord Mayor of London">
       mayor
      </a>
      with a captain, appointed by the Crown.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The London elite were themselves fighting out a vicious, internal battle for political power.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As a result, in 1381 the ruling classes in London were unstable and divided.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Rural communities, particularly in the south-east, were unhappy with the operation of
      <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">
       serfdom
      </a>
      and the use of the local manorial courts to exact traditional fines and levies, not least because the same landowners who ran these courts also often acted as enforcers of the unpopular labour laws or as royal judges.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many of the village elites refused to take up positions in local government and began to frustrate the operation of the courts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Animals seized by the courts began to be retaken by their owners, and legal officials were assaulted.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some started to advocate the creation of independent village communities, respecting traditional laws but separate from the hated legal system centred in London.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As the historian
      <a href="/wiki/Miri_Rubin" title="Miri Rubin">
       Miri Rubin
      </a>
      describes, for many, "the problem was not the country's laws, but those charged with applying and safeguarding them".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Concerns were raised about these changes in society.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/William_Langland" title="William Langland">
       William Langland
      </a>
      wrote the poem
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Piers_Plowman" title="Piers Plowman">
        Piers Plowman
       </a>
      </i>
      in the years before 1380, praising peasants who respected the law and worked hard for their lords, but complaining about greedy, travelling labourers demanding higher wages.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The poet
      <a href="/wiki/John_Gower" title="John Gower">
       John Gower
      </a>
      feared England might see an uprising similar to the French
      <a href="/wiki/Jacquerie" title="Jacquerie">
       Jacquerie
      </a>
      revolt of 1358, in which the peasants had risen up against their masters.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There was a
      <a href="/wiki/Moral_panic" title="Moral panic">
       moral panic
      </a>
      about the threat posed by newly arrived workers in the towns and the possibility that servants might turn against their masters.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      New legislation was introduced in 1359 to deal with migrants, existing
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conspiracy_(crime)" title="Conspiracy (crime)">
       conspiracy laws
      </a>
      were more widely applied and the
      <a href="/wiki/Treason_Act_1351" title="Treason Act 1351">
       treason laws
      </a>
      were extended to include servants or wives who betrayed their masters and husbands.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By the 1370s, there were fears that if the French invaded England, the rural classes might side with the invaders.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dyer_2009_285_18-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dyer_2009_285-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The discontent began to give way to open protest. In 1377, the "
      <a href="/wiki/Great_Rumour" title="Great Rumour">
       Great Rumour
      </a>
      " occurred in south-east and south-west England.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Rural workers organised themselves and refused to work for their lords, arguing that, according to the
      <a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">
       Domesday Book
      </a>
      , they were exempted from such requests.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The workers made unsuccessful appeals to the law courts and the King.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There were also widespread urban tensions, particularly in London, where John of Gaunt narrowly escaped being lynched.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The troubles increased again in 1380, with protests and disturbances across northern England and in the western towns of
      <a href="/wiki/Shrewsbury" title="Shrewsbury">
       Shrewsbury
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Bridgwater" title="Bridgwater">
       Bridgwater
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      An uprising occurred in York, during which John de Gisborne, the city's mayor, was removed from office, and fresh tax riots followed in early 1381.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There was a great storm in England during May 1381, which many felt to prophesy future change and upheaval, adding further to the disturbed mood.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Events">
       Events
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Events">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Outbreak_of_revolt">
       Outbreak of revolt
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Outbreak of revolt">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Essex_and_Kent">
       Essex and Kent
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Essex and Kent">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The revolt of 1381 broke out in
      <a href="/wiki/Essex" title="Essex">
       Essex
      </a>
      , following the arrival of John Bampton to investigate non-payment of the poll tax on 30 May.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P73_74-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P73-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Bampton was a member of Parliament, a Justice of the Peace and well-connected with royal circles.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P73_74-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P73-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He based himself in
      <a href="/wiki/Brentwood,_Essex" title="Brentwood, Essex">
       Brentwood
      </a>
      and summoned representatives from the neighbouring villages of
      <a href="/wiki/Corringham,_Essex" title="Corringham, Essex">
       Corringham
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Fobbing" title="Fobbing">
       Fobbing
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Stanford-le-Hope" title="Stanford-le-Hope">
       Stanford-le-Hope
      </a>
      to explain and make good the shortfalls on 1 June.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P73_74-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P73-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The villagers appear to have arrived well-organised, and armed with old bows and sticks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sumption_2009_420_75-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sumption_2009_420-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Bampton first interrogated the people of Fobbing, whose representative,
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Baker_(Peasants%27_Revolt_leader)" title="Thomas Baker (Peasants' Revolt leader)">
       Thomas Baker
      </a>
      , declared that his village had already paid their taxes, and that no more money would be forthcoming.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sumption_2009_420_75-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sumption_2009_420-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      When Bampton and two sergeants attempted to arrest Baker, violence broke out.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P73_74-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P73-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Bampton escaped and retreated to London, but three of his clerks and several of the Brentwood townsfolk who had agreed to act as jurors were killed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bealknap" title="Robert Bealknap">
       Robert Bealknap
      </a>
      , the Chief Justice of the
      <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Common_Pleas_(England)" title="Court of Common Pleas (England)">
       Court of Common Pleas
      </a>
      , who was probably already holding court in the area, was empowered to arrest and deal with the perpetrators.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Longbowmen.jpg">
        <img alt="Medieval painting" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="433" data-file-width="394" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Longbowmen.jpg/170px-Longbowmen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Longbowmen.jpg/255px-Longbowmen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Longbowmen.jpg/340px-Longbowmen.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Longbowmen.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Peasant longbowmen at practice, from the Luttrell Psalter, c. 132040
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      By the next day, the revolt was rapidly growing.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P74_78-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P74-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The villagers spread the news across the region, and John Geoffrey, a local bailiff, rode between Brentwood and
      <a href="/wiki/Chelmsford" title="Chelmsford">
       Chelmsford
      </a>
      , rallying support.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P74_78-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P74-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On 4 June, the rebels gathered at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bocking,_Essex" title="Bocking, Essex">
       Bocking
      </a>
      , where their future plans seem to have been discussed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Essex rebels, possibly a few thousand strong, advanced towards London, some probably travelling directly and others via Kent.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P74_78-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P74-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      One group, under the leadership of
      <a href="/wiki/John_Wrawe" title="John Wrawe">
       John Wrawe
      </a>
      , a former
      <a href="/wiki/Chaplain" title="Chaplain">
       chaplain
      </a>
      , marched north towards the neighbouring county of Suffolk, with the intention of raising a revolt there.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
       <a href="#cite_note-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Revolt also flared in neighbouring
      <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">
       Kent
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P75_81-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P75-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Sir
      <a href="/wiki/Simon_de_Burley" title="Simon de Burley">
       Simon de Burley
      </a>
      , a close associate of both Edward III and the young Richard, had claimed that a man in Kent, called Robert Belling, was an escaped serf from one of his estates.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P75_81-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P75-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Burley sent two sergeants to
      <a href="/wiki/Gravesend" title="Gravesend">
       Gravesend
      </a>
      , where Belling was living, to reclaim him.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P75_81-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P75-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Gravesend's local bailiffs and Belling tried to negotiate a solution under which Burley would accept a sum of money in return for dropping his case, but this failed and Belling was taken away to be imprisoned at
      <a href="/wiki/Rochester_Castle" title="Rochester Castle">
       Rochester Castle
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P75_81-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P75-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A furious group of local people gathered at
      <a href="/wiki/Dartford" title="Dartford">
       Dartford
      </a>
      , possibly on 5 June, to discuss the matter.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      From there the rebels travelled to
      <a href="/wiki/Maidstone" title="Maidstone">
       Maidstone
      </a>
      , where they stormed the gaol, and then onto
      <a href="/wiki/Rochester,_Kent" title="Rochester, Kent">
       Rochester
      </a>
      on 6 June.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Faced by the angry crowds, the constable in charge of Rochester Castle surrendered it without a fight and Belling was freed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P76_84-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P76-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Some of the Kentish crowds now dispersed, but others continued.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P76_84-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P76-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      From this point, they appear to have been led by
      <a href="/wiki/Wat_Tyler" title="Wat Tyler">
       Wat Tyler
      </a>
      , whom the
      <i>
       Anonimalle Chronicle
      </i>
      suggests was elected their leader at a large gathering at Maidstone on 7 June.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002Sumption2009P421_85-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002Sumption2009P421-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Relatively little is known about Tyler's former life; chroniclers suggest that he was from Essex, had served in France as an archer and was a charismatic and capable leader.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002Sumption2009P421_85-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002Sumption2009P421-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Several chroniclers believe that he was responsible for shaping the political aims of the revolt.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some also mention a
      <a href="/wiki/Jack_Straw_(rebel_leader)" title="Jack Straw (rebel leader)">
       Jack Straw
      </a>
      as a leader among the Kentish rebels during this phase in the revolt, but it is uncertain if this was a real person, or a pseudonym for Wat Tyler or John Wrawe.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Tyler and the Kentish men advanced to
      <a href="/wiki/Canterbury" title="Canterbury">
       Canterbury
      </a>
      , entering the
      <a href="/wiki/Canterbury_city_walls" title="Canterbury city walls">
       walled city
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Canterbury_Castle" title="Canterbury Castle">
       castle
      </a>
      without resistance on 10 June.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The rebels deposed the absent Archbishop of Canterbury, Sudbury, and made the cathedral monks swear loyalty to their cause.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They attacked properties in the city with links to the hated royal council, and searched the city for suspected enemies, dragging the suspects out of their houses and executing them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The city gaol was opened and the prisoners freed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Tyler then persuaded a few thousand of the rebels to leave Canterbury and advance with him on London the next morning.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P78_94-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P78-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="March_on_the_capital">
       March on the capital
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: March on the capital">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Ball_encouraging_Wat_Tyler_rebels_from_ca_1470_MS_of_Froissart_Chronicles_in_BL.jpg">
        <img alt="Medieval painting" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1120" data-file-width="1424" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/John_Ball_encouraging_Wat_Tyler_rebels_from_ca_1470_MS_of_Froissart_Chronicles_in_BL.jpg/220px-John_Ball_encouraging_Wat_Tyler_rebels_from_ca_1470_MS_of_Froissart_Chronicles_in_BL.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/John_Ball_encouraging_Wat_Tyler_rebels_from_ca_1470_MS_of_Froissart_Chronicles_in_BL.jpg/330px-John_Ball_encouraging_Wat_Tyler_rebels_from_ca_1470_MS_of_Froissart_Chronicles_in_BL.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/John_Ball_encouraging_Wat_Tyler_rebels_from_ca_1470_MS_of_Froissart_Chronicles_in_BL.jpg/440px-John_Ball_encouraging_Wat_Tyler_rebels_from_ca_1470_MS_of_Froissart_Chronicles_in_BL.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_Ball_encouraging_Wat_Tyler_rebels_from_ca_1470_MS_of_Froissart_Chronicles_in_BL.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        15th-century representation of the cleric
        <a href="/wiki/John_Ball_(priest)" title="John Ball (priest)">
         John Ball
        </a>
        encouraging the rebels; Wat Tyler is shown in red, front left
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Kentish advance on London appears to have been coordinated with the movement of the rebels in Essex,
      <a href="/wiki/Suffolk" title="Suffolk">
       Suffolk
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Norfolk" title="Norfolk">
       Norfolk
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P78_94-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P78-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Their forces were armed with weapons including sticks, battle axes, old swords and bows.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        91
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
       <a href="#cite_note-97">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Along their way, they encountered
      <a href="/wiki/Joan_of_Kent" title="Joan of Kent">
       Lady Joan
      </a>
      , the King's mother, who was travelling back to the capital to avoid being caught up in the revolt; she was mocked but otherwise left unharmed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P78_94-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P78-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Kentish rebels reached
      <a href="/wiki/Blackheath,_London" title="Blackheath, London">
       Blackheath
      </a>
      , just south-east of the capital, on 12 June.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P78_94-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P78-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
       <a href="#cite_note-99">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Word of the revolt reached the King at
      <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Castle" title="Windsor Castle">
       Windsor Castle
      </a>
      on the night of 10 June.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P78_94-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P78-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He travelled by boat down the
      <a href="/wiki/River_Thames" title="River Thames">
       River Thames
      </a>
      to London the next day, taking up residence in the powerful fortress of the
      <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">
       Tower of London
      </a>
      for safety, where he was joined by his mother, Archbishop Sudbury, the
      <a href="/wiki/Lord_High_Treasurer" title="Lord High Treasurer">
       Lord High Treasurer
      </a>
      Sir
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hales" title="Robert Hales">
       Robert Hales
      </a>
      , the Earls of
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_FitzAlan,_11th_Earl_of_Arundel" title="Richard FitzAlan, 11th Earl of Arundel">
       Arundel
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/William_de_Montacute,_2nd_Earl_of_Salisbury" title="William de Montacute, 2nd Earl of Salisbury">
       Salisbury
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_de_Beauchamp,_12th_Earl_of_Warwick" title="Thomas de Beauchamp, 12th Earl of Warwick">
       Warwick
      </a>
      and several other senior nobles.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
       <a href="#cite_note-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        94
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A delegation, headed by
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Brinton" title="Thomas Brinton">
       Thomas Brinton
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Rochester" title="Bishop of Rochester">
       Bishop of Rochester
      </a>
      , was sent out from London to negotiate with the rebels and persuade them to return home.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P78_94-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P78-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      At Blackheath,
      <a href="/wiki/John_Ball_(priest)" title="John Ball (priest)">
       John Ball
      </a>
      gave a famous sermon to the assembled Kentishmen.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sumption_2009_423_101-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sumption_2009_423-101">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Ball was a well-known priest and radical preacher from Kent, who was by now closely associated with Tyler.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
       <a href="#cite_note-102">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        96
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Chroniclers' accounts vary as to how he came to be involved in the revolt; he may have been released from Maidstone gaol by the crowds, or might have been already at liberty when the revolt broke out.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
       <a href="#cite_note-103">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        97
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Ball rhetorically asked the crowds "When Adam delved and Eve span, who was then a gentleman?" and promoted the rebel slogan "With King Richard and the true commons of England".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sumption_2009_423_101-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sumption_2009_423-101">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The phrases emphasised the rebel opposition to the continuation of serfdom and to the hierarchies of the Church and State that separated the subject from the King, while stressing that they were loyal to the monarchy and, unlike the King's advisers, were "true" to Richard.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
       <a href="#cite_note-104">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        98
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The rebels rejected proposals from the Bishop of Rochester that they should return home, and instead prepared to march on.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P78_94-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P78-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Discussions took place in the Tower of London about how to deal with the revolt.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P78_94-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P78-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The King had only a few troops at hand, in the form of the castle's garrison, his immediate bodyguard and, at most, several hundred soldiers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
       <a href="#cite_note-105">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        99
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
       <a href="#cite_note-107">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many of the more experienced military commanders were in France, Ireland and Germany, and the nearest major military force was in the north of England, guarding against a potential Scottish invasion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
       <a href="#cite_note-108">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        101
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Resistance in the provinces was also complicated by English law, which stated that only the King could summon local militias or lawfully execute rebels and criminals, leaving many local lords unwilling to attempt to suppress the uprisings on their own authority.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
       <a href="#cite_note-109">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        102
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Since the Blackheath negotiations had failed, the decision was taken that the King himself should meet the rebels, at
      <a href="/wiki/Greenwich" title="Greenwich">
       Greenwich
      </a>
      , on the south side of the Thames.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
       <a href="#cite_note-110">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        103
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Guarded by four barges of soldiers, Richard sailed from the Tower on the morning of 13 June, where he was met on the other side by the rebel crowds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P79_111-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P79-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        104
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The negotiations failed, as Richard was unwilling to come ashore and the rebels refused to enter discussions until he did.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P79_111-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P79-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        104
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Richard returned across the river to the Tower.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated79_112-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated79-112">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        105
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Events_in_London">
       Events in London
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Events in London">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Entry_to_the_city">
       Entry to the city
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Entry to the city">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_London,_1381_labelled.png">
        <img alt="Map of London" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="791" data-file-width="1080" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Map_of_London%2C_1381_labelled.png/300px-Map_of_London%2C_1381_labelled.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Map_of_London%2C_1381_labelled.png/450px-Map_of_London%2C_1381_labelled.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Map_of_London%2C_1381_labelled.png/600px-Map_of_London%2C_1381_labelled.png 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_London,_1381_labelled.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Map of London in 1381: A Clerkenwell; B Priory of St. John; C Smithfield; D Newgate and Fleet Prisons; E The Savoy Palace; F The Temple; G- Black Friars; H Aldgate; I Mile End; J Westminster; K Southwark; L Marshalsea Prison; M London Bridge; N Tower of London
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The rebels began to cross from
      <a href="/wiki/Southwark" title="Southwark">
       Southwark
      </a>
      onto
      <a href="/wiki/London_Bridge" title="London Bridge">
       London Bridge
      </a>
      on the afternoon of 13 June.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated79_112-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated79-112">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        105
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The defences on London Bridge were opened from the inside, either in sympathy for the rebel cause or out of fear, and the rebels advanced into the city.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
       <a href="#cite_note-113">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        106
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
       <a href="#cite_note-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      At the same time, the rebel force from Essex made its way towards
      <a href="/wiki/Aldgate" title="Aldgate">
       Aldgate
      </a>
      on the north side of the city.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
       <a href="#cite_note-116">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        108
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The rebels swept west through the centre of the city, and Aldgate was opened to let the rest of the rebels in.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
       <a href="#cite_note-117">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        109
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Kentish rebels had assembled a wide-ranging list of people whom they wanted the King to hand over for execution.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P79_111-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P79-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        104
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It included national figures, such as John of Gaunt, Archbishop Sudbury and Hales; other key members of the royal council; officials, such as Bealknap and Bampton who had intervened in Kent; and other hated members of the wider royal circle.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P79_111-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P79-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        104
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      When they reached the Marshalsea Prison in Southwark, they tore it apart.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
       <a href="#cite_note-118">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        110
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By now the Kent and Essex rebels had been joined by many rebellious Londoners.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
       <a href="#cite_note-119">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        111
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Prison" title="Fleet Prison">
       Fleet
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Newgate_Prison" title="Newgate Prison">
       Newgate Prisons
      </a>
      were attacked by the crowds, and the rebels also targeted houses belonging to Flemish immigrants.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
       <a href="#cite_note-120">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        112
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On the north side of London, the rebels approached
      <a href="/wiki/Smithfield,_London" title="Smithfield, London">
       Smithfield
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Clerkenwell_Priory" title="Clerkenwell Priory">
       Clerkenwell Priory
      </a>
      , the headquarters of the
      <a href="/wiki/Knights_Hospitaller" title="Knights Hospitaller">
       Knights Hospitaller
      </a>
      which was headed by Hales.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P83_121-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P83-121">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        113
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The priory was destroyed, along with the nearby manor.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P83_121-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P83-121">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        113
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Heading west along
      <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Street" title="Fleet Street">
       Fleet Street
      </a>
      , the rebels attacked
      <a href="/wiki/Temple,_London" title="Temple, London">
       the Temple
      </a>
      , a complex of legal buildings and offices owned by the Hospitallers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P84_122-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P84-122">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The contents, books and paperwork were brought out and burned in the street, and the buildings systematically demolished.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P84_122-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P84-122">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Meanwhile,
      <a href="/wiki/John_Fordham" title="John Fordham">
       John Fordham
      </a>
      , the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keeper_of_the_Privy_Seal" title="Keeper of the Privy Seal">
       Keeper of the Privy Seal
      </a>
      and one of the men on the rebels' execution list, narrowly escaped when the crowds ransacked his accommodation but failed to notice he was still in the building.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P84_122-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P84-122">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Next to be attacked along Fleet Street was the
      <a href="/wiki/Savoy_Palace" title="Savoy Palace">
       Savoy Palace
      </a>
      , a huge, luxurious building belonging to John of Gaunt.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002PP85_123-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002PP85-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        115
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to the chronicler
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_Knighton" title="Henry Knighton">
       Henry Knighton
      </a>
      it contained "such quantities of vessels and silver plate, without counting the parcel-gilt and solid gold, that five carts would hardly suffice to carry them"; official estimates placed the value of the contents at around 10,000.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002PP85_123-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002PP85-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        115
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
