      The interior was systematically destroyed by the rebels, who burnt the soft furnishings, smashed the precious metal work, crushed the gems, set fire to the Duke's records and threw the remains into the Thames and the city drains.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002PP85_123-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002PP85-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        115
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Almost nothing was stolen by the rebels, who declared themselves to be "zealots for truth and justice, not thieves and robbers".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124">
       <a href="#cite_note-124">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        116
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The remains of the building were then set alight.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
       <a href="#cite_note-125">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        117
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the evening, rebel forces gathered outside the Tower of London, from where the King watched the fires burning across the city.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
       <a href="#cite_note-126">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        118
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Taking_the_Tower_of_London">
       Taking the Tower of London
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Taking the Tower of London">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Towrlndn.JPG">
        <img alt="Medieval painting" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="992" data-file-width="876" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Towrlndn.JPG/170px-Towrlndn.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Towrlndn.JPG/255px-Towrlndn.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Towrlndn.JPG/340px-Towrlndn.JPG 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Towrlndn.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Late 15th-century depiction of the
        <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">
         Tower of London
        </a>
        and its
        <a href="/wiki/Keep" title="Keep">
         keep
        </a>
        , the
        <a href="/wiki/White_Tower_(Tower_of_London)" title="White Tower (Tower of London)">
         White Tower
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      On the morning of 14 June, the crowd continued west along the Thames, burning the houses of officials around
      <a href="/wiki/Westminster" title="Westminster">
       Westminster
      </a>
      and opening the Westminster gaol.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P88_127-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P88-127">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        119
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They then moved back into central London, setting fire to more buildings and storming Newgate Prison.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P88_127-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P88-127">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        119
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The hunt for Flemings continued, and those with Flemish-sounding accents were killed, including the royal adviser,
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_Lyons_(Warden_of_the_Mint)" title="Richard Lyons (Warden of the Mint)">
       Richard Lyons
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
       <a href="#cite_note-128">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        120
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130">
       <a href="#cite_note-130">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In one city
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ward_(country_subdivision)" title="Ward (country subdivision)">
       ward
      </a>
      , the bodies of 40 executed Flemings were piled up in the street, and at the Church of
      <a href="/wiki/St_Martin_Vintry" title="St Martin Vintry">
       St Martin Vintry
      </a>
      , popular with the Flemish, 35 of the community were killed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131">
       <a href="#cite_note-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        122
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Historian
      <a href="/wiki/Rodney_Hilton" title="Rodney Hilton">
       Rodney Hilton
      </a>
      argues that these attacks may have been coordinated by the weavers' guilds of London, who were commercial competitors of the Flemish weavers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hilton_1995_195_132-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hilton_1995_195-132">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        123
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Isolated inside the Tower, the royal government was in a state of shock at the turn of events.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133">
       <a href="#cite_note-133">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        124
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The King left the castle that morning and made his way to negotiate with the rebels at
      <a href="/wiki/Mile_End" title="Mile End">
       Mile End
      </a>
      in east London, taking only a very small bodyguard with him.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134">
       <a href="#cite_note-134">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        125
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The King left Sudbury and Hales behind in the Tower, either for their own safety or because Richard had decided it would be safer to distance himself from his unpopular ministers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135">
       <a href="#cite_note-135">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        126
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Along the way, several Londoners accosted the King to complain about alleged injustices.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136">
       <a href="#cite_note-136">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        127
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      It is uncertain who spoke for the rebels at Mile End, and Wat Tyler may not have been present on this occasion, but they appear to have put forward their various demands to the King, including the surrender of the hated officials on their lists for execution; the abolition of serfdom and unfree tenure; "that there should be no law within the realm save the law of Winchester", and a general amnesty for the rebels.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137">
       <a href="#cite_note-137">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        128
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is unclear precisely what was meant by the law of
      <a href="/wiki/Winchester" title="Winchester">
       Winchester
      </a>
      , but it probably referred to the rebel ideal of self-regulating village communities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138">
       <a href="#cite_note-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        129
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">
       <a href="#cite_note-140">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Richard issued charters announcing the abolition of serfdom, which immediately began to be disseminated around the country.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141">
       <a href="#cite_note-141">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He declined to hand over any of his officials, apparently instead promising that he would personally implement any justice that was required.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142">
       <a href="#cite_note-142">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        132
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      While Richard was at Mile End, the Tower was taken by the rebels.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P99_143-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P99-143">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        133
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A force of rebels, separate from those operating under Tyler at Mile End, approached the castle, possibly in the late morning.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P99_143-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P99-143">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        133
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144">
       <a href="#cite_note-144">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The gates were open to receive Richard on his return and a crowd of around 400 rebels entered the fortress, encountering no resistance, possibly because the guards were terrified by them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145">
       <a href="#cite_note-145">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        134
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Once inside, the rebels began to hunt down their key targets, and found Archbishop Sudbury and Robert Hales in the chapel of the White Tower.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sumptio2009PP427_146-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sumptio2009PP427-146">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        135
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Along with William Appleton, John of Gaunt's physician, and John Legge, a royal sergeant, they were taken out to
      <a href="/wiki/Tower_Hill" title="Tower Hill">
       Tower Hill
      </a>
      and beheaded.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sumptio2009PP427_146-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sumptio2009PP427-146">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        135
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Their heads were paraded around the city, before being affixed to London Bridge.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
       <a href="#cite_note-147">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        136
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The rebels found John of Gaunt's son, the future
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_England" title="Henry IV of England">
       Henry IV
      </a>
      , and were about to execute him as well, when John Ferrour, one of the royal guards, successfully interceded on his behalf.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">
       <a href="#cite_note-148">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        137
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The rebels also discovered Lady Joan and
      <a href="/wiki/Joan_Holland,_Duchess_of_Brittany" title="Joan Holland, Duchess of Brittany">
       Joan Holland
      </a>
      , Richard's sister, in the castle but let them go unharmed after making fun of them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149">
       <a href="#cite_note-149">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The castle was thoroughly looted of armour and royal paraphernalia.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saul1999P69_150-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Saul1999P69-150">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        139
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the aftermath of the attack, Richard did not return to the Tower but instead travelled from Mile End to the Great Wardrobe, one of his royal houses in
      <a href="/wiki/Blackfriars,_London" title="Blackfriars, London">
       Blackfriars
      </a>
      , part of south-west London.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151">
       <a href="#cite_note-151">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        140
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There he appointed the military commander Richard FitzAlan, the
      <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Arundel" title="Earl of Arundel">
       Earl of Arundel
      </a>
      , to replace Sudbury as Chancellor, and began to make plans to regain an advantage over the rebels the following day.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152">
       <a href="#cite_note-152">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        141
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many of the Essex rebels now began to disperse, content with the King's promises, leaving Tyler and the Kentish forces the most significant faction in London.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153">
       <a href="#cite_note-153">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        142
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Tyler's men moved around the city that evening, seeking out and killing John of Gaunt's employees, foreigners and anyone associated with the legal system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154">
       <a href="#cite_note-154">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        143
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Smithfield">
       Smithfield
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Smithfield">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DeathWatTylerFull.jpg">
        <img alt="Medieval painting" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1130" data-file-width="1422" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/DeathWatTylerFull.jpg/220px-DeathWatTylerFull.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/DeathWatTylerFull.jpg/330px-DeathWatTylerFull.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/DeathWatTylerFull.jpg/440px-DeathWatTylerFull.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DeathWatTylerFull.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Late 14th-century depiction of William Walworth killing Wat Tyler; the King is represented twice, watching events unfold (left) and addressing the crowd (right).
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      On 15 June the royal government and the remaining rebels, who were unsatisfied with the charters granted the previous day, agreed to meet at Smithfield, just outside the city walls.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155">
       <a href="#cite_note-155">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        144
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      London remained in confusion, with various bands of rebels roaming the city independently.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saul1999P69_150-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Saul1999P69-150">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        139
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Richard prayed at Westminster Abbey, before setting out for the meeting in the late afternoon.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156">
       <a href="#cite_note-156">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        145
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The chronicler accounts of the encounter all vary on matters of detail, but agree on the broad sequence of events.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157">
       <a href="#cite_note-157">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        146
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The King and his party, at least 200 strong and including
      <a href="/wiki/Man-at-arms" title="Man-at-arms">
       men-at-arms
      </a>
      , positioned themselves outside
      <a href="/wiki/St_Bartholomew-the-Great" title="St Bartholomew-the-Great">
       St Bartholomew's Priory
      </a>
      to the east of Smithfield, and the thousands of rebels massed along the western end.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158">
       <a href="#cite_note-158">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        147
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160">
       <a href="#cite_note-160">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Richard probably called Tyler forwards from the crowd to meet him, and Tyler greeted the King with what the royal party considered excessive familiarity, terming Richard his "brother" and promising him his friendship.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161">
       <a href="#cite_note-161">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        149
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Richard queried why Tyler and the rebels had not yet left London following the signing of the charters the previous day, but this brought an angry rebuke from Tyler, who requested that a further charter be drawn up.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162">
       <a href="#cite_note-162">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        150
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The rebel leader rudely demanded refreshment and, once this had been provided, attempted to leave.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002PP106_163-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002PP106-163">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        151
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      An argument then broke out between Tyler and some of the royal servants.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002PP106_163-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002PP106-163">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        151
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Mayor of London,
      <a href="/wiki/William_Walworth" title="William Walworth">
       William Walworth
      </a>
      , stepped forward to intervene, Tyler made some motion towards the King, and the royal soldiers leapt in.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164">
       <a href="#cite_note-164">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        152
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Either Walworth or Richard ordered Tyler to be arrested, Tyler attempted to attack the Mayor, and Walworth responded by stabbing Tyler.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002PP106_163-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002PP106-163">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        151
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Ralph Standish, a royal
      <a href="/wiki/Squire" title="Squire">
       squire
      </a>
      , then repeatedly stabbed Tyler with his sword, mortally injuring him.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P107_165-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P107-165">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        153
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The situation was now precarious and violence appeared likely as the rebels prepared to unleash a volley of arrows.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P107_165-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P107-165">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        153
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Richard rode forwards towards the crowd and persuaded them to follow him away from Smithfields, to
      <a href="/wiki/Clerkenwell" title="Clerkenwell">
       Clerkenwell Fields
      </a>
      , defusing the situation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P107_165-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P107-165">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        153
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Walworth meanwhile began to regain control of the situation, backed by reinforcements from the city.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P107_165-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P107-165">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        153
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Tyler's head was cut off and displayed on a pole and, with their leader dead and the royal government now backed by the London militia, the rebel movement began to collapse.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166">
       <a href="#cite_note-166">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        154
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Richard promptly knighted Walworth and his leading supporters for their services.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P107_165-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P107-165">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        153
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Wider_revolt">
       Wider revolt
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Wider revolt">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_England">
       Eastern England
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Eastern England">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abbeygate_In_Bury_St_Edmunds.jpg">
        <img alt="Photograph" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1696" data-file-width="1279" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Abbeygate_In_Bury_St_Edmunds.jpg/170px-Abbeygate_In_Bury_St_Edmunds.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Abbeygate_In_Bury_St_Edmunds.jpg/255px-Abbeygate_In_Bury_St_Edmunds.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Abbeygate_In_Bury_St_Edmunds.jpg/340px-Abbeygate_In_Bury_St_Edmunds.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abbeygate_In_Bury_St_Edmunds.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The Abbey Gate of
        <a href="/wiki/Bury_St_Edmunds_Abbey" title="Bury St Edmunds Abbey">
         Bury St Edmunds Abbey
        </a>
        , stormed by the rebels on 13 June
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      While the revolt was unfolding in London, John Wrawe led his force into Suffolk.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167">
       <a href="#cite_note-167">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        155
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Wrawe had considerable influence over the development of the revolt across eastern England, where there may have been almost as many rebels as in the London revolt.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168">
       <a href="#cite_note-168">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        156
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The authorities put up very little resistance to the revolt: the major nobles failed to organise defences, key fortifications fell easily to the rebels and the local militias were not mobilised.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169">
       <a href="#cite_note-169">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        157
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As in London and the south-east, this was in part due to the absence of key military leaders and the nature of English law, but any locally recruited men might also have proved unreliable in the face of a popular uprising.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170">
       <a href="#cite_note-170">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        158
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On 12 June, Wrawe attacked Sir Richard Lyons' property at Overhall, advancing on to
      <a href="/wiki/Cavendish,_Suffolk" title="Cavendish, Suffolk">
       Cavendish
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Bury_St_Edmunds" title="Bury St Edmunds">
       Bury St Edmunds
      </a>
      in west Suffolk the next day, gathering further support as they went.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171">
       <a href="#cite_note-171">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        159
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      John Cambridge, the Prior of the wealthy
      <a href="/wiki/Bury_St_Edmunds_Abbey" title="Bury St Edmunds Abbey">
       Bury St Edmunds Abbey
      </a>
      , was disliked in the town, and Wrawe allied himself with the townspeople and stormed the abbey.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172">
       <a href="#cite_note-172">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        160
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Prior escaped, but was found two days later and beheaded.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173">
       <a href="#cite_note-173">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        161
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A small band of rebels marched north to
      <a href="/wiki/Thetford" title="Thetford">
       Thetford
      </a>
      to extort
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protection_money" title="Protection money">
       protection money
      </a>
      from the town, and another group tracked down Sir
      <a href="/wiki/John_Cavendish" title="John Cavendish">
       John Cavendish
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chief_Justice_of_England_and_Wales" title="Lord Chief Justice of England and Wales">
       Chief Justice of the King's Bench
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_(education)" title="Chancellor (education)">
       Chancellor
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">
       University of Cambridge
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174">
       <a href="#cite_note-174">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        162
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Cavendish was caught in
      <a href="/wiki/Lakenheath" title="Lakenheath">
       Lakenheath
      </a>
      and killed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175">
       <a href="#cite_note-175">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        163
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      John Battisford and Thomas Sampson independently led a revolt near
      <a href="/wiki/Ipswich" title="Ipswich">
       Ipswich
      </a>
      on 14 June.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P126_176-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P126-176">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        164
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They took the town without opposition and looted the properties of the
      <a href="/wiki/Archdeacon" title="Archdeacon">
       archdeacon
      </a>
      and local tax officials.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P126_176-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P126-176">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        164
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The violence spread out further, with attacks on many properties and the burning of the local court records.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177">
       <a href="#cite_note-177">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        165
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      One official, Edmund Lakenheath, was forced to flee from the Suffolk coast by boat.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178">
       <a href="#cite_note-178">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        166
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Revolt began to stir in
      <a href="/wiki/St_Albans" title="St Albans">
       St Albans
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Hertfordshire" title="Hertfordshire">
       Hertfordshire
      </a>
      late on 13 June, when news broke of the events in London.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P113_179-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P113-179">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        167
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There had been long-running disagreements in St Albans between the town and the local
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St_Albans_Abbey" title="St Albans Abbey">
       abbey
      </a>
      , which had extensive privileges in the region.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180">
       <a href="#cite_note-180">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        168
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On 14 June, protesters met with the Abbot, Thomas de la Mare, and demanded their freedom from the abbey.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P113_179-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P113-179">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        167
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A group of townsmen under the leadership of
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=William_Grindecobbe&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="William Grindecobbe (page does not exist)">
       William Grindecobbe
      </a>
      traveled to London, where they appealed to the King for the rights of the abbey to be abolished.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181">
       <a href="#cite_note-181">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        169
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Wat Tyler, then still in control of the city, granted them authority in the meantime to take direct action against the abbey.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182">
       <a href="#cite_note-182">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        170
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Grindecobbe and the rebels returned to St Albans, where they found the Prior had already fled.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183">
       <a href="#cite_note-183">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        171
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The rebels broke open the abbey gaol, destroyed the fences marking out the abbey lands and burnt the abbey records in the town square.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184">
       <a href="#cite_note-184">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        172
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They then forced Thomas de la Mare to surrender the abbey's rights in a charter on 16 June.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185">
       <a href="#cite_note-185">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        173
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The revolt against the abbey spread out over the next few days, with abbey property and financial records being destroyed across the county.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186">
       <a href="#cite_note-186">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        174
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OldCCav.JPG">
        <img alt="Photograph" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/OldCCav.JPG/220px-OldCCav.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/OldCCav.JPG/330px-OldCCav.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/OldCCav.JPG/440px-OldCCav.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OldCCav.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Corpus_Christi_College,_Cambridge" title="Corpus Christi College, Cambridge">
         Corpus Christi College
        </a>
        's Old Court, attacked by the rebels on 15 June
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      On 15 June, revolt broke out in
      <a href="/wiki/Cambridgeshire" title="Cambridgeshire">
       Cambridgeshire
      </a>
      , led by elements of Wrawe's Suffolk rebellion and some local men, such as John Greyston, who had been involved in the events in London and had returned to his home county to spread the revolt, and Geoffrey Cobbe and John Hanchach, members of the local gentry.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P127_187-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P127-187">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        175
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The University of Cambridge, staffed by priests and enjoying special royal privileges, was widely hated by the other inhabitants of the town.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P127_187-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P127-187">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        175
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A revolt backed by the Mayor of Cambridge broke out with the university as its main target.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P127_187-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P127-187">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        175
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The rebels ransacked
      <a href="/wiki/Corpus_Christi_College,_Cambridge" title="Corpus Christi College, Cambridge">
       Corpus Christi College
      </a>
      , which had connections to John of Gaunt, and the
      <a href="/wiki/Church_of_St_Mary_the_Great,_Cambridge" title="Church of St Mary the Great, Cambridge">
       University's church
      </a>
      , and attempted to execute the University
      <a href="/wiki/Bedel" title="Bedel">
       bedel
      </a>
      , who escaped.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188">
       <a href="#cite_note-188">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        176
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The university's library and archives were burnt in the centre of the town.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189">
       <a href="#cite_note-189">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        177
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The next day, the university was forced to negotiate a new charter, giving up its royal privileges.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190">
       <a href="#cite_note-190">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        178
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Revolt then spread north from Cambridge toward
      <a href="/wiki/Ely,_Cambridgeshire" title="Ely, Cambridgeshire">
       Ely
      </a>
      , where the gaol was opened and the local Justice of the Peace executed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191">
       <a href="#cite_note-191">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        179
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In Norfolk, the revolt was led by Geoffrey Litster, a weaver, and Sir Roger Bacon, a local lord with ties to the Suffolk rebels.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192">
       <a href="#cite_note-192">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        180
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Litster began sending out messengers across the county in a call to arms on 14 June, and isolated outbreaks of violence occurred.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193">
       <a href="#cite_note-193">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        181
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The rebels assembled on 17 June outside
      <a href="/wiki/Norwich" title="Norwich">
       Norwich
      </a>
      and killed Sir Robert Salle, who was in charge of the city defences and had attempted to negotiate a settlement.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002Powell1896P29_194-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002Powell1896P29-194">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        182
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The people of the town then opened the gates to let the rebels in.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002Powell1896P29_194-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002Powell1896P29-194">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        182
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They began looting buildings and killed Reginald Eccles, a local official.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195">
       <a href="#cite_note-195">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        183
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/William_de_Ufford,_2nd_Earl_of_Suffolk" title="William de Ufford, 2nd Earl of Suffolk">
       William de Ufford
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Suffolk" title="Earl of Suffolk">
       Earl of Suffolk
      </a>
      fled his estates and travelled in disguise to London.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P131_196-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P131-196">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        184
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The other leading members of the local gentry were captured and forced to play out the roles of a royal household, working for Litster.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P131_196-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P131-196">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        184
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Violence spread out across the county, as gaols were opened, Flemish immigrants killed, court records burned, and property looted and destroyed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197">
       <a href="#cite_note-197">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        185
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_and_western_England">
       Northern and western England
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Northern and western England">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Gower_world_Vox_Clamantis.jpg">
        <img alt="Medieval painting" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1171" data-file-width="757" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/John_Gower_world_Vox_Clamantis.jpg/170px-John_Gower_world_Vox_Clamantis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/John_Gower_world_Vox_Clamantis.jpg/255px-John_Gower_world_Vox_Clamantis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/John_Gower_world_Vox_Clamantis.jpg/340px-John_Gower_world_Vox_Clamantis.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_Gower_world_Vox_Clamantis.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        An illustration from
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Vox_Clamantis" title="Vox Clamantis">
          Vox Clamantis
         </a>
        </i>
        by John Gower, a poem which described the revolt
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Revolts also occurred across the rest of England, particularly in the cities of the north, traditionally centres of political unrest.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198">
       <a href="#cite_note-198">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        186
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the town of
      <a href="/wiki/Beverley" title="Beverley">
       Beverley
      </a>
      , violence broke out between the richer mercantile elite and the poorer townspeople during May.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199">
       <a href="#cite_note-199">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        187
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By the end of the month the rebels had taken power and replaced the former town administration with their own.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200">
       <a href="#cite_note-200">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        188
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The rebels attempted to enlist the support of
      <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Neville" title="Alexander Neville">
       Alexander Neville
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_York" title="Archbishop of York">
       Archbishop of York
      </a>
      , and in June forced the former town government to agree to arbitration through Neville.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201">
       <a href="#cite_note-201">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        189
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Peace was restored in June 1382 but tensions continued to simmer for many years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202">
       <a href="#cite_note-202">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        190
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Word of the troubles in the south-east spread north, slowed by the poor communication links of medieval England.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P121_203-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P121-203">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        191
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Leicester" title="Leicester">
       Leicester
      </a>
      , where John of Gaunt had a substantial
      <a href="/wiki/Leicester_Castle" title="Leicester Castle">
       castle
      </a>
      , warnings arrived of a force of rebels advancing on the city from
      <a href="/wiki/Lincolnshire" title="Lincolnshire">
       Lincolnshire
      </a>
      , who were intent on destroying the castle and its contents.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P121_203-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P121-203">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        191
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The mayor and the town mobilised their defences, including a local militia, but the rebels never arrived.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204">
       <a href="#cite_note-204">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        192
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      John of Gaunt was in
      <a href="/wiki/Berwick-upon-Tweed" title="Berwick-upon-Tweed">
       Berwick
      </a>
      when word reached him on 17 June of the revolt.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205">
       <a href="#cite_note-205">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        193
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Not knowing that Wat Tyler had by now been killed, John of Gaunt placed his castles in Yorkshire and Wales on alert.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002PP143_206-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002PP143-206">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        194
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Fresh rumours, many of them incorrect, continued to arrive in Berwick, suggesting widespread rebellions across the west and east of England and the looting of the ducal household in Leicester; rebel units were even said to be hunting for the Duke himself.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002PP143_206-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002PP143-206">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        194
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Gaunt began to march to
      <a href="/wiki/Bamburgh_Castle" title="Bamburgh Castle">
       Bamburgh Castle
      </a>
      , but then changed course and diverted north into Scotland, only returning south once the fighting was over.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207">
       <a href="#cite_note-207">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        195
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      News of the initial events in London also reached York around 17 June, and attacks at once broke out on the properties of the Dominican friars, the Franciscan friaries and other religious institutions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208">
       <a href="#cite_note-208">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        196
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Violence continued over the coming weeks, and on 1 July a group of armed men, under the command of John de Gisbourne, forced their way into the city and attempted to seize control.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dobson1987P122_209-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dobson1987P122-209">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        197
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The mayor, Simon de Quixlay, gradually began to reclaim authority, but order was not properly restored until 1382.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dobson1987P122_209-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dobson1987P122-209">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        197
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The news of the southern revolt reached Scarborough where riots broke out against the ruling elite on 23 June, with the rebels dressed in white hoods with a red tail at the back.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210">
       <a href="#cite_note-210">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        198
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Members of the local government were deposed from office, and one tax collector was nearly lynched.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211">
       <a href="#cite_note-211">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        199
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By 1382 the elite had re-established power.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212">
       <a href="#cite_note-212">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        200
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the
      <a href="/wiki/Somerset" title="Somerset">
       Somerset
      </a>
      town of Bridgwater, revolt broke out on 19 June, led by Thomas Ingleby and Adam Brugge.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213">
       <a href="#cite_note-213">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        201
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The crowds attacked the local
      <a href="/wiki/Augustinians" title="Augustinians">
       Augustine
      </a>
      house and forced their master to give up his local privileges and pay a ransom.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214">
       <a href="#cite_note-214">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        202
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The rebels then turned on the properties of John Sydenham, a local merchant and official, looting his manor and burning paperwork, before executing Walter Baron, a local man.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215">
       <a href="#cite_note-215">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        203
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Ilchester" title="Ilchester">
       Ilchester
      </a>
      gaol was stormed, and one unpopular prisoner executed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216">
       <a href="#cite_note-216">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        204
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Suppression">
       Suppression
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Suppression">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Henry_le_Despenser_2.jpg">
        <img alt="Photograph" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2816" data-file-width="1602" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Henry_le_Despenser_2.jpg/170px-Henry_le_Despenser_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Henry_le_Despenser_2.jpg/255px-Henry_le_Despenser_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Henry_le_Despenser_2.jpg/340px-Henry_le_Despenser_2.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Henry_le_Despenser_2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A 14th-century carving of
        <a href="/wiki/Henry_le_Despenser" title="Henry le Despenser">
         Henry le Despenser
        </a>
        , the victor of the
        <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_North_Walsham" title="Battle of North Walsham">
         Battle of North Walsham
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The royal suppression of the revolt began shortly after the death of Wat Tyler on 15 June.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217">
       <a href="#cite_note-217">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        205
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Sir
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Knolles" title="Robert Knolles">
       Robert Knolles
      </a>
      , Sir
      <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Brembre" title="Nicholas Brembre">
       Nicholas Brembre
      </a>
      and Sir Robert Launde were appointed to restore control in the capital.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218">
       <a href="#cite_note-218">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        206
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A summons was put out for soldiers, probably around 4,000 men were mustered in London, and expeditions to the other troubled parts of the country soon followed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219">
       <a href="#cite_note-219">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        207
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The revolt in East Anglia was independently suppressed by
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_le_Despenser" title="Henry le Despenser">
       Henry le Despenser
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Norwich" title="Bishop of Norwich">
       Bishop of Norwich
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P131_196-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P131-196">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        184
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Henry was in
      <a href="/wiki/Stamford,_Lincolnshire" title="Stamford, Lincolnshire">
       Stamford
      </a>
      in Lincolnshire when the revolt broke out, and when he found out about it he marched south with eight men-at-arms and a small force of archers, gathering more forces as he went.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220">
       <a href="#cite_note-220">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        208
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He marched first to
      <a href="/wiki/Peterborough" title="Peterborough">
       Peterborough
      </a>
      , where he routed the local rebels and executed any he could capture, including some who had taken shelter in the local abbey.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221">
       <a href="#cite_note-221">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        209
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He then headed south-east via
      <a href="/wiki/Huntingdon" title="Huntingdon">
       Huntingdon
      </a>
      and Ely, reached Cambridge on 19 June, and then headed further into the rebel-controlled areas of Norfolk.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222">
       <a href="#cite_note-222">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        210
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Henry reclaimed Norwich on 24 June, before heading out with a company of men to track down the rebel leader, Geoffrey Litster.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223">
       <a href="#cite_note-223">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        211
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The two forces met at the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_North_Walsham" title="Battle of North Walsham">
       Battle of North Walsham
      </a>
      on 25 or 26 June; the Bishop's forces triumphed and Litster was captured and executed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224">
       <a href="#cite_note-224">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        212
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Henry's quick action was essential to the suppression of the revolt in East Anglia, but he was very unusual in taking matters into his own hands in this way, and his execution of the rebels without royal sanction was illegal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225">
       <a href="#cite_note-225">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        213
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On 17 June, the King dispatched his half-brother
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Holland,_2nd_Earl_of_Kent" title="Thomas Holland, 2nd Earl of Kent">
       Thomas Holland
      </a>
      and Sir Thomas Trivet to Kent with a small force to restore order.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P136_226-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P136-226">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        214
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They held courts at Maidstone and Rochester.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P136_226-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P136-226">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        214
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      William de Ufford, the Earl of Suffolk, returned to his county on 23 June, accompanied by a force of 500 men.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227">
       <a href="#cite_note-227">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        215
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He quickly subdued the area and was soon holding court in Mildenhall, where many of the accused were sentenced to death.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228">
       <a href="#cite_note-228">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        216
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He moved on into Norfolk on 6 July, holding court in Norwich,
      <a href="/wiki/Great_Yarmouth" title="Great Yarmouth">
       Great Yarmouth
      </a>
      and
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hacking,_Norfolk&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hacking, Norfolk (page does not exist)">
       Hacking
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P136_226-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P136-226">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        214
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hugh, Lord la Zouche, led the legal proceedings against the rebels in Cambridgeshire.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P136_226-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P136-226">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        214
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In St Albans, the Abbot arrested William Grindecobbe and his main supporters.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229">
       <a href="#cite_note-229">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        217
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On 20 June, the King's uncle,
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_of_Woodstock,_1st_Duke_of_Gloucester" title="Thomas of Woodstock, 1st Duke of Gloucester">
       Thomas of Woodstock
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Tresilian" title="Robert Tresilian">
       Robert Tresilian
      </a>
      , the replacement Chief Justice, were given special commissions across the whole of England.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P136_226-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P136-226">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        214
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thomas oversaw court cases in Essex, backed up by a substantial military force as resistance was continuing and the county was still in a state of unrest.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230">
       <a href="#cite_note-230">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        218
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Richard himself visited Essex, where he met with a rebel delegation seeking confirmation of the grants the King had given at Mile End.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saul1999P74_231-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Saul1999P74-231">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        219
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Richard rejected them, allegedly telling them that "rustics you were and rustics you are still. You will remain in bondage, not as before, but incomparably harsher".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saul1999P74_231-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Saul1999P74-231">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        219
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233">
       <a href="#cite_note-233">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Tresilian soon joined Thomas, and carried out 31 executions in Chelmsford, then travelled to St Albans in July for further court trials, which appear to have utilised dubious techniques to ensure convictions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234">
       <a href="#cite_note-234">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        221
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thomas went on to Gloucester with 200 soldiers to suppress the unrest there.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P137_235-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P137-235">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        222
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_Percy,_1st_Earl_of_Northumberland" title="Henry Percy, 1st Earl of Northumberland">
       Henry Percy
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Northumberland" title="Earl of Northumberland">
       Earl of Northumberland
      </a>
      , was tasked to restore order to Yorkshire.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P137_235-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P137-235">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        222
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A wide range of laws were invoked in the process of the suppression, from general
      <a href="/wiki/Treason" title="Treason">
       treason
      </a>
      to charges of book burning or demolishing houses, a process complicated by the relatively narrow definition of treason at the time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236">
       <a href="#cite_note-236">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        223
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The use of informants and denunciations became common, causing fear to spread across the country; by November at least 1,500 people had been executed or killed in battle.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237">
       <a href="#cite_note-237">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        224
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many of those who had lost property in the revolt attempted to seek legal compensation, and John of Gaunt made particular efforts to track down those responsible for destroying his Savoy Palace.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P138_238-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P138-238">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        225
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most had only limited success, as the defendants were rarely willing to attend court.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P138_238-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P138-238">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        225
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The last of these cases was resolved in 1387.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P138_238-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P138-238">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        225
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The rebel leaders were quickly rounded up.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239">
       <a href="#cite_note-239">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        226
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A rebel leader by the name of Jack Straw was captured in London and executed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P139_240-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P139-240">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        227
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242">
       <a href="#cite_note-242">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      John Ball was caught in Coventry, tried in St Albans, and executed on 15 July.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243">
       <a href="#cite_note-243">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        229
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Grindecobbe was also tried and executed in St Albans.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P139_240-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P139-240">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        227
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      John Wrawe was tried in London; he probably
      <a href="/wiki/Turn_state%27s_evidence" title="Turn state's evidence">
       gave evidence
      </a>
      against 24 of his colleagues in the hope of a pardon, but was sentenced to be executed by being
      <a href="/wiki/Hanged,_drawn_and_quartered" title="Hanged, drawn and quartered">
       hanged, drawn and quartered
      </a>
      on 6 May 1382.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244">
       <a href="#cite_note-244">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        230
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Sir Roger Bacon was probably arrested before the final battle in Norfolk, and was tried and imprisoned in the Tower of London before finally being pardoned by the Crown.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245">
       <a href="#cite_note-245">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        231
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As of September 1381, Thomas Ingleby of Bridgwater had successfully evaded the authorities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246">
       <a href="#cite_note-246">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        232
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">
       Aftermath
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Aftermath">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Richard_II_King_of_England.jpg">
        <img alt="Medieval painting" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="279" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Richard_II_King_of_England.jpg/170px-Richard_II_King_of_England.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Richard_II_King_of_England.jpg/255px-Richard_II_King_of_England.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a1/Richard_II_King_of_England.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Richard_II_King_of_England.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Late 14th-century portrait of Richard II
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The royal government and Parliament began to re-establish the normal processes of government after the revolt; as the historian
      <a href="/wiki/Michael_Postan" title="Michael Postan">
       Michael Postan
      </a>
      describes, the uprising was in many ways a "passing episode".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247">
       <a href="#cite_note-247">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        233
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On 30 June, the King ordered England's serfs to return to their previous conditions of service, and on 2 July the royal charters signed under duress during the rising were formally revoked.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P136_226-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P136-226">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        214
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Parliament met in November to discuss the events of the year and how best to respond to their challenges.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248">
       <a href="#cite_note-248">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        234
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The revolt was blamed on the misconduct of royal officials, who, it was argued, had been excessively greedy and overbearing.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249">
       <a href="#cite_note-249">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        235
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Commons stood behind the existing labour laws, but requested changes in the royal council, which Richard granted.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250">
       <a href="#cite_note-250">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        236
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Richard also granted general pardons to those who had executed rebels without due process, to all men who had remained loyal, and to all those who had rebelled with the exception of the men of Bury St Edmunds, any men who had been involved in the killing of the King's advisers, and those who were still on the run from prison.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251">
       <a href="#cite_note-251">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        237
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Despite the violence of the suppression, the government and local lords were relatively circumspect in restoring order after the revolt, and continued to be worried about fresh revolts for several decades.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252">
       <a href="#cite_note-252">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        238
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Few lords took revenge on their peasants except through the legal processes of the courts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253">
       <a href="#cite_note-253">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        239
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Low-level unrest continued for several more years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254">
       <a href="#cite_note-254">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        240
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In September 1382 there was trouble in Norfolk, involving an apparent plot against the Bishop of Norwich, and in March the following year there was an investigation into a plot to kill the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sheriff_of_Devon" title="Sheriff of Devon">
       sheriff of Devon
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255">
       <a href="#cite_note-255">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        241
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      When negotiating rents with their landlords, peasants alluded to the memory of the revolt and the threat of violence.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dyer_2009_291_256-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dyer_2009_291-256">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        242
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      There were no further attempts by Parliament to impose a poll tax or to reform England's fiscal system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257">
       <a href="#cite_note-257">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        243
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Commons instead concluded at the end of 1381 that the military effort on the Continent should be "carefully but substantially reduced".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258">
       <a href="#cite_note-258">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        244
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Unable to raise fresh taxes, the government had to curtail its foreign policy and military expeditions and began to examine the options for peace.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259">
       <a href="#cite_note-259">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        245
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The institution of serfdom declined after 1381, but primarily for economic rather than political reasons.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260">
       <a href="#cite_note-260">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        246
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Rural wages continued to increase, and lords increasingly sold their serfs' freedom in exchange for cash, or converted traditional forms of tenure to new
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leasehold" title="Leasehold">
       leasehold
      </a>
      arrangements.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261">
       <a href="#cite_note-261">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        247
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      During the 15th century the institution vanished in England.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dyer_2009_291_256-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dyer_2009_291-256">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        242
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Rebels">
       Rebels
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Rebels">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reeve_and_Serfs.jpg">
        <img alt="Medieval painting" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="461" data-file-width="800" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Reeve_and_Serfs.jpg/300px-Reeve_and_Serfs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Reeve_and_Serfs.jpg/450px-Reeve_and_Serfs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Reeve_and_Serfs.jpg/600px-Reeve_and_Serfs.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Reeve_and_Serfs.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        14th-century rural scene of reeve directing serfs, from the
        <a href="/wiki/Queen_Mary_Psalter" title="Queen Mary Psalter">
         Queen Mary Psalter
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Chroniclers primarily described the rebels as rural serfs, using broad, derogatory
      <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
       Latin
      </a>
      terms such as
      <i>
       serviles rustici
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       servile genu
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       rustictas
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hilton1995Crane1992P202_262-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hilton1995Crane1992P202-262">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        248
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some chroniclers, including Knighton, also noted the presence of runaway apprentices, artisans and others, sometimes terming them the "lesser commons".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hilton1995Crane1992P202_262-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hilton1995Crane1992P202-262">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        248
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The evidence from the court records following the revolt, albeit biased in various ways, similarly shows the involvement of a much broader community, and the earlier perception that the rebels were only constituted of unfree serfs is now rejected.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263">
       <a href="#cite_note-263">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        249
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265">
       <a href="#cite_note-265">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The rural rebels came from a wide range of backgrounds, but typically they were, as the historian
      <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Dyer" title="Christopher Dyer">
       Christopher Dyer
      </a>
      describes, "people well below the ranks of the gentry, but who mainly held some land and goods", and not the very poorest in society, who formed a minority of the rebel movement.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266">
       <a href="#cite_note-266">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        251
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many had held positions of authority in local village governance, and these seem to have provided leadership to the revolt.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267">
       <a href="#cite_note-267">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        252
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some were artisans, including, as the historian Rodney Hilton lists, "carpenters, sawyers, masons, cobblers, tailors, weavers, fullers, glovers, hosiers, skinners, bakers, butchers, innkeepers, cooks and a lime-burner".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268">
       <a href="#cite_note-268">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        253
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They were predominantly male, but with some women in their ranks.
