      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269">
       <a href="#cite_note-269">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        254
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The rebels were typically illiterate; only between 5 and 15 per cent of England could read during this period.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270">
       <a href="#cite_note-270">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        255
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They also came from a broad range of local communities, including at least 330 south-eastern villages.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271">
       <a href="#cite_note-271">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        256
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Many of the rebels had urban backgrounds, and the majority of those involved in the events of London were probably local townsfolk rather than peasants.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272">
       <a href="#cite_note-272">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        257
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In some cases, the townsfolk who joined the revolt were the urban poor, attempting to gain at the expense of the local elites.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273">
       <a href="#cite_note-273">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        258
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In London, for example, the urban rebels appear to have largely been the poor and unskilled.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hilton_1995_195_132-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hilton_1995_195-132">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        123
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other urban rebels were part of the elite, such as at York where the protesters were typically prosperous members of the local community, while in some instances, townsfolk allied themselves with the rural population, as at Bury St Edmunds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274">
       <a href="#cite_note-274">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        259
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In other cases, such as Canterbury, the influx of population from the villages following the Black Death made any distinction between urban and rural less meaningful.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275">
       <a href="#cite_note-275">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        260
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The vast majority of those involved in the revolt of 1381 were not represented in Parliament and were excluded from its decision-making.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276">
       <a href="#cite_note-276">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        261
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In a few cases the rebels were led or joined by relatively prosperous members of the gentry, such as Sir Roger Bacon in Norfolk.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277">
       <a href="#cite_note-277">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        262
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some of them later claimed to have been forced to join the revolt by the rebels.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278">
       <a href="#cite_note-278">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        263
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Clergy also formed part of the revolt; as well as the more prominent leaders, such as John Ball or John Wrawe, nearly 20 are mentioned in the records of the revolt in the south-east.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279">
       <a href="#cite_note-279">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        264
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some were pursuing local grievances, some were disadvantaged and suffering relative poverty, and others appear to have been motivated by strong radical beliefs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280">
       <a href="#cite_note-280">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        265
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Many of those involved in the revolt used pseudonyms, particularly in the letters sent around the country to encourage support and fresh uprisings.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281">
       <a href="#cite_note-281">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        266
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They were used both to avoid incriminating particular individuals and to allude to popular values and stories.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282">
       <a href="#cite_note-282">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        267
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      One popular assumed name was Piers Plowman, taken from the main character in William Langland's poem.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283">
       <a href="#cite_note-283">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        268
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Jack was also a widely used rebel pseudonym, and historians Steven Justice and Carter Revard suggest that this may have been because it resonated with the Jacques of the French Jacquerie revolt several decades earlier.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284">
       <a href="#cite_note-284">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        269
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">
       Legacy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Legacy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">
       Historiography
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Historiography">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_William_Stubbs_by_Hubert_von_Herkomer.jpeg">
        <img alt="Portrait painting of an older grey-haired man with grey whiskers clad in black and sitting in a chair" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="944" data-file-width="779" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Portrait_of_William_Stubbs_by_Hubert_von_Herkomer.jpeg/170px-Portrait_of_William_Stubbs_by_Hubert_von_Herkomer.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Portrait_of_William_Stubbs_by_Hubert_von_Herkomer.jpeg/255px-Portrait_of_William_Stubbs_by_Hubert_von_Herkomer.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Portrait_of_William_Stubbs_by_Hubert_von_Herkomer.jpeg/340px-Portrait_of_William_Stubbs_by_Hubert_von_Herkomer.jpeg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_William_Stubbs_by_Hubert_von_Herkomer.jpeg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Historian
        <a href="/wiki/William_Stubbs" title="William Stubbs">
         William Stubbs
        </a>
        , who considered the revolt "one of the most portentous events in the whole of our history", painted by
        <a href="/wiki/Hubert_von_Herkomer" title="Hubert von Herkomer">
         Hubert von Herkomer
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hilton1987P2_285-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Hilton1987P2-285">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          270
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Contemporary chroniclers of the events in the revolt have formed an important source for historians. The chroniclers were biased against the rebel cause and typically portrayed the rebels, in the words of the historian Susan Crane, as "beasts, monstrosities or misguided fools".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286">
       <a href="#cite_note-286">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        271
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      London chroniclers were also unwilling to admit the role of ordinary Londoners in the revolt, preferring to place the blame entirely on rural peasants from the south-east.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strohm2008P201_287-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Strohm2008P201-287">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        272
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Among the key accounts was the anonymous
      <i>
       Anonimalle Chronicle
      </i>
      , whose author appears to have been part of the royal court and an eye-witness to many of the events in London.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288">
       <a href="#cite_note-288">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        273
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The chronicler
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Walsingham" title="Thomas Walsingham">
       Thomas Walsingham
      </a>
      was present for much of the revolt, but focused his account on the terror of the social unrest and was extremely biased against the rebels.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289">
       <a href="#cite_note-289">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        274
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The events were recorded in France by
      <a href="/wiki/Jean_Froissart" title="Jean Froissart">
       Jean Froissart
      </a>
      , the author of the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Froissart%27s_Chronicles" title="Froissart's Chronicles">
        Chronicles
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290">
       <a href="#cite_note-290">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        275
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He had well-placed sources close to the revolt, but was inclined to elaborate the known facts with colourful stories.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291">
       <a href="#cite_note-291">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        276
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      No sympathetic accounts of the rebels survive.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strohm_2008_203_98-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Strohm_2008_203-98">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      At the end of the 19th century there was a surge in historical interest in the Peasants' Revolt, spurred by the contemporary growth of the labour and socialist movements.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292">
       <a href="#cite_note-292">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        277
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Work by
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_Oman" title="Charles Oman">
       Charles Oman
      </a>
      , Edgar Powell, Andr Rville and
      <a href="/wiki/G._M._Trevelyan" title="G. M. Trevelyan">
       G. M. Trevelyan
      </a>
      established the course of the revolt.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293">
       <a href="#cite_note-293">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        278
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By 1907 the accounts of the chroniclers were all widely available in print and the main public records concerning the events had been identified.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294">
       <a href="#cite_note-294">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        279
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Rville began to use the legal indictments that had been used against suspected rebels after the revolt as a fresh source of historical information, and over the next century extensive research was carried out into the local economic and social history of the revolt, using scattered local sources across south-east England.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295">
       <a href="#cite_note-295">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        280
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Interpretations of the revolt have changed over the years. 17th-century historians, such John Smyth, established the idea that the revolt had marked the end of unfree labour and serfdom in England.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hilton1987P2_285-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hilton1987P2-285">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        270
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      19th-century historians such as
      <a href="/wiki/William_Stubbs" title="William Stubbs">
       William Stubbs
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Thorold_Rogers" title="Thorold Rogers">
       Thorold Rogers
      </a>
      reinforced this conclusion, Stubbs describing it as "one of the most portentous events in the whole of our history".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hilton1987P2_285-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hilton1987P2-285">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        270
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the 20th century, this interpretation was increasingly challenged by historians such as
      <a href="/wiki/May_McKisack" title="May McKisack">
       May McKisack
      </a>
      , Michael Postan and Richard Dobson, who revised the impact of the revolt on further political and economic events in England.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296">
       <a href="#cite_note-296">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        281
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Mid-20th century Marxist historians were both interested in, and generally sympathetic to, the rebel cause, a trend culminating in Hilton's 1973 account of the uprising, set against the context of wider peasant revolts across Europe during the period.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297">
       <a href="#cite_note-297">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        282
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Peasants' Revolt has received more academic attention than any other medieval revolt, and this research has been interdisciplinary, involving historians, literary scholars and international collaboration.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298">
       <a href="#cite_note-298">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        283
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The name "the Peasants' Revolt" emerged in the 18th and early 19th centuries, and its first recorded use by historians was in
      <a href="/wiki/John_Richard_Green" title="John Richard Green">
       John Richard Green
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       Short History of the English People
      </i>
      in 1874.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strohm2008P201_287-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Strohm2008P201-287">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        272
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Contemporary chronicles did not give the revolt a specific title, and the term "peasant" did not appear in the English language until the 15th century.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strohm2008P201_287-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Strohm2008P201-287">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        272
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The title has been critiqued by modern historians such as Miri Rubin and Paul Strohm, both on the grounds that many in the movements were not peasants, and that the events more closely resemble a prolonged protest or rising, rather than a revolt or rebellion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299">
       <a href="#cite_note-299">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        284
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Popular_culture">
       Popular culture
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Popular culture">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:William.Morris.John.Ball.trimmed.jpg">
        <img alt="Engraved illustration" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1773" data-file-width="1592" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/William.Morris.John.Ball.trimmed.jpg/170px-William.Morris.John.Ball.trimmed.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/William.Morris.John.Ball.trimmed.jpg/255px-William.Morris.John.Ball.trimmed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/William.Morris.John.Ball.trimmed.jpg/340px-William.Morris.John.Ball.trimmed.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:William.Morris.John.Ball.trimmed.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Illustration from title page to
        <a href="/wiki/William_Morris" title="William Morris">
         William Morris
        </a>
        's
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/A_Dream_of_John_Ball" title="A Dream of John Ball">
          A Dream of John Ball
         </a>
        </i>
        (1888), by
        <a href="/wiki/Edward_Burne-Jones" title="Edward Burne-Jones">
         Edward Burne-Jones
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Peasants' Revolt became a popular literary subject.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300">
       <a href="#cite_note-300">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        285
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The poet John Gower, who had close ties to officials involved in the suppression of the revolt, wrote his famous poem
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Vox_Clamantis" title="Vox Clamantis">
        Vox Clamantis
       </a>
      </i>
      in the decades after the revolt, condemning the rebels and likening them to wild animals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301">
       <a href="#cite_note-301">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        286
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">
       Geoffrey Chaucer
      </a>
      , who lived in Aldgate and may have been in London during the revolt, used the rebel killing of Flemings as a metaphor for wider disorder in
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Nun%27s_Priest%27s_Tale" title="The Nun's Priest's Tale">
        The Nun's Priest's Tale
       </a>
      </i>
      part of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Canterbury_Tales" title="The Canterbury Tales">
        The Canterbury Tales
       </a>
      </i>
      , parodying Gower's poem.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302">
       <a href="#cite_note-302">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        287
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Chaucer otherwise made no reference to the revolt in his work, possibly because as he was a client of the King it would have been politically unwise to discuss it.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303">
       <a href="#cite_note-303">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        288
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      William Langland, the author of the poem
      <i>
       Piers Plowman
      </i>
      , which had been widely used by the rebels, made various changes to its text after the revolt in order to distance himself from their cause.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304">
       <a href="#cite_note-304">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        289
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The revolt formed the basis for the late 16th-century play,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Life_and_Death_of_Jack_Straw" title="The Life and Death of Jack Straw">
        The Life and Death of Jack Straw
       </a>
      </i>
      , possibly written by
      <a href="/wiki/George_Peele" title="George Peele">
       George Peele
      </a>
      and probably originally designed for production in the city's guild pageants.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305">
       <a href="#cite_note-305">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        290
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It portrays Jack Straw as a tragic figure, being led into wrongful rebellion by John Ball, making clear political links between the instability of late-Elizabethan England and the 14th century.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306">
       <a href="#cite_note-306">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        291
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The story of the revolt was used in pamphlets during the
      <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">
       English Civil War
      </a>
      of the 17th century, and formed part of
      <a href="/wiki/John_Cleveland" title="John Cleveland">
       John Cleveland
      </a>
      's early history of the war.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307">
       <a href="#cite_note-307">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        292
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It was deployed as a cautionary account in political speeches during the 18th century, and a
      <a href="/wiki/Chapbook" title="Chapbook">
       chapbook
      </a>
      entitled
      <i>
       The History of Wat Tyler and Jack Strawe
      </i>
      proved popular during the
      <a href="/wiki/Jacobite_risings" title="Jacobite risings">
       Jacobite risings
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_War_of_Independence" title="American War of Independence">
       American War of Independence
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308">
       <a href="#cite_note-308">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        293
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">
       Thomas Paine
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Burke" title="Edmund Burke">
       Edmund Burke
      </a>
      argued over the lessons to be drawn from the revolt, Paine expressing sympathy for the rebels and Burke condemning the violence.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309">
       <a href="#cite_note-309">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        294
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Romantic_poetry" title="Romantic poetry">
       Romantic poet
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Southey" title="Robert Southey">
       Robert Southey
      </a>
      based his 1794 play
      <i>
       Wat Tyler
      </i>
      on the events, taking a radical and pro-rebel perspective.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310">
       <a href="#cite_note-310">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        295
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      As the historian Michael Postan describes, the revolt became famous "as a landmark in social development and [as] a typical instance of working-class revolt against oppression", and was widely used in 19th and 20th century
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socialist" title="Socialist">
       socialist
      </a>
      literature.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ortenberg1981P79_311-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ortenberg1981P79-311">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        296
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/William_Morris" title="William Morris">
       William Morris
      </a>
      built on Chaucer in his novel
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/A_Dream_of_John_Ball" title="A Dream of John Ball">
        A Dream of John Ball
       </a>
      </i>
      , published in 1888, creating a narrator who was openly sympathetic to the peasant cause, albeit a 19th-century persona taken back to the 14th century by a dream.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312">
       <a href="#cite_note-312">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        297
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The story ends with a prophecy that socialist ideals will one day be successful.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313">
       <a href="#cite_note-313">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        298
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In turn, this representation of the revolt influenced Morris's
      <a href="/wiki/Utopian_socialism" title="Utopian socialism">
       utopian socialist
      </a>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/News_from_Nowhere" title="News from Nowhere">
        News from Nowhere
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314">
       <a href="#cite_note-314">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        299
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Florence_Converse" title="Florence Converse">
       Florence Converse
      </a>
      used the revolt in her novel
      <i>
       Long Will
      </i>
      in 1903.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ortenberg1981P79_311-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ortenberg1981P79-311">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        296
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Later 20th century socialists continued to draw parallels between the revolt and contemporary political struggles, including during the arguments over the introduction of the
      <a href="/wiki/Community_Charge" title="Community Charge">
       Community Charge
      </a>
      in the United Kingdom during the 1980s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ortenberg1981P79_311-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ortenberg1981P79-311">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        296
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Conspiracy_theory" title="Conspiracy theory">
       Conspiracy theorists
      </a>
      , including writer
      <a href="/wiki/John_J._Robinson" title="John J. Robinson">
       John Robinson
      </a>
      , have attempted to explain alleged flaws in mainstream historical accounts of the events of 1381, such as the speed with which the rebellion was coordinated.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315">
       <a href="#cite_note-315">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        300
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Theories include that the revolt was led by a secret,
      <a href="/wiki/Occult" title="Occult">
       occult
      </a>
      organisation called "the Great Society", said to be an offshoot of the order of the
      <a href="/wiki/Knights_Templar" title="Knights Templar">
       Knights Templar
      </a>
      destroyed in 1312, or that the fraternity of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freemasons" title="Freemasons">
       Freemasons
      </a>
      was covertly involved in organising the revolt.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316">
       <a href="#cite_note-316">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        301
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318">
       <a href="#cite_note-318">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">
       See also
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: See also">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Popular_revolt_in_late-medieval_Europe" title="Popular revolt in late-medieval Europe">
        Popular revolt in late-medieval Europe
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Jack_Cade" title="Jack Cade">
        Jack Cade
       </a>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
       Notes
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peasants%27_Revolt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Notes">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
      <ol class="references">
       <li id="cite_note-28">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-28">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         It is impossible to accurately compare 14th century and modern prices or incomes. For comparison, the income of a typical nobleman such as
         <a href="/wiki/Richard_le_Scrope,_1st_Baron_Scrope_of_Bolton" title="Richard le Scrope, 1st Baron Scrope of Bolton">
          Richard le Scrope
         </a>
         was around 600 a year, while only six
         <a href="/wiki/Earl" title="Earl">
          earls
         </a>
         in the kingdom enjoyed incomes of over 5,000 a year.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
          <a href="#cite_note-27">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           27
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-36">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-36">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         For comparison, the wage for an unskilled labourer in Essex in 1380 was around three pence a day.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
          <a href="#cite_note-35">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           34
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-51">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-51">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         The Marshalsea Court was originally intended to provide justice for the royal household and those doing business with it, travelling with the King around the country and having authority covering 12 miles (19km) around the monarch. The monarchs of the 14th century were increasingly based in London, resulting in the Marshalsea Court taking up semi-permanent business in the capital. Successive monarchs used the court to exercise royal power, often at the expense of the City of London's Corporation.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
          <a href="#cite_note-50">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           48
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-89">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-89">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Walsingham highlights the role of a "Jack Straw", and is supported by Froissart, although Knighton argues that this was a pseudonym; other chroniclers fail to mention him at all. The historian Friedrich Brie popularised the argument in favour of the pseudonym in 1906. Modern historians recognise Tyler as the primary leader, and are doubtful about the role of "Jack Straw".
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
          <a href="#cite_note-88">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           85
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-97">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-97">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Military historian Jonathan Sumption considers this description of the rebels' weaponry, drawn from the chronicler Thomas Walsingham, as reliable; literary historian Stephen Justice is less certain, noting the sarcastic manner in which Walsingham mocks the rebels' old and dilapidated arms, including their bows "reddened with age and smoke."
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
          <a href="#cite_note-96">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           92
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-99">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-99">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Historian Andrew Prescott has critiqued these timings, arguing that it would have been unlikely that so many rebels could have advanced so fast on London, given the condition of the medieval road networks.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strohm_2008_203_98-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-Strohm_2008_203-98">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           93
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-107">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-107">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Chronicler figures for the King's immediate forces in London vary; Henry Knighton argues that the King had between 150180 men in the Tower of London, Thomas Walsingham suggests 1,200. These were probably over-estimates, and historian Alastair Dunn assesses that only a skeleton force was present; Jonathan Sumption judges that around 150 men-at-arms were present, and some archers.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
          <a href="#cite_note-106">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           100
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-115">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-115">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         It is uncertain who opened the defences at London Bridge and Aldgate. After the revolt three aldermen, John Horn, Walter Sibil and William Tongue, were put on trial by the authorities, but it is unclear how far these accusations were motivated by the post-conflict London politics. The historian Nigel Saul is doubtful of their guilt in collaborating with the rebels. Rodney Hilton suggests that they may have opened the gates in order to buy time and so prevent the destruction of their city, although he prefers the theory that the London crowds forced the gates to be opened. Jonathan Sumption similarly argues that the aldermen were forced to open the gates in the face of popular pressure.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
          <a href="#cite_note-114">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           107
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-130">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-130">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         The royal adviser Richard Lyons was believed to have Flemish origins, although he was also unpopular in his own right as a result of his role in government.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129">
          <a href="#cite_note-129">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           121
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-140">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-140">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         The rebel call for a return to the "law of Winchester" has been much debated. One theory is that it was another term for the
         <a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">
          Domesday Book
         </a>
         of
         <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">
          William I
         </a>
         , which was believed to provide protection for particular groups of tenants. Another is that it referred to the
         <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_Winchester" title="Statute of Winchester">
          Statute of Winchester
         </a>
         in 1285, which allowed for the enforcement of local law through armed village communities, and which had been cited in more recent legislation on the criminal law. The creation of special justices and royal officials during the 14th century were seen as eroding these principles.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139">
          <a href="#cite_note-139">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           130
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-144">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-144">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Most chroniclers stated that the force that attacked the Tower of London was separate to that operating under Tyler's command at Mile End; only the
         <i>
          Anonimalle Chronicle
         </i>
         links them to Tyler. The timing of the late morning attack relies on the account of the
         <i>
          Westminster Chronicle
         </i>
         .
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn2002P99_143-2">
          <a href="#cite_note-Dunn2002P99-143">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           133
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-160">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-160">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         The primary sources for the events at Smithfield are the
         <i>
          Anonimalle Chronicle
         </i>
         , Thomas Walsingham, Jean Froissart, Henry Knighton and the
         <i>
          Westminster Chronicler
         </i>
         . There are minor differences in their accounts of events. Froissart suggests that Wat Tyler intended to capture the King and kill the royal party, and that Tyler initiated the engagement with Richard in order to carry out this plan. The
         <i>
          Anonimalle Chronicle
         </i>
         and Walsingham both go into some, if varying, detail as to the rebels' demands. Walsingham and Knighton wrote that Tyler, rather than being about to depart at the end of his discussions with Richard, appeared to be about to kill the King, triggering the royal response. Walsingham differs from the other chroniclers in giving a key role in the early part of the encounter to Sir John Newton.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159">
          <a href="#cite_note-159">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           148
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-233">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-233">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         The "rustics" quotation from Richard II is from the chronicler Thomas Walsingham, and should be treated with caution. Historian Dan Jones suspects that although Richard no doubt despised the rebels, the language itself may have been largely invented by Walsingham.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232">
          <a href="#cite_note-232">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           220
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-242">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-242">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         As noted above, questions exist over Jack Straw's identity. The chronicler
         <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Walsingham" title="Thomas Walsingham">
          Thomas Walsingham
         </a>
         attributes a long confession to the Jack Straw executed in London, but the reliability of this is questioned by historians: Rodney Hilton refers to it as "somewhat dubious", while Alastair Dunn considers it to be essentially a fabrication. There are no reliable details of the trial or execution.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241">
          <a href="#cite_note-241">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           228
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-265">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-265">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Historian Sylvia Federico notes the dangers in treating the pardons lists simplistically, given the tendency for some innocent individuals to acquire pardons for additional security, and the tendency for cases to be brought against individuals for local, non-political reasons.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264">
          <a href="#cite_note-264">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           250
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-318">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-318">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         The term "the Great Society" emerges from indictments against the rebels, in which references were made the
         <i>
          magne societatis
         </i>
         . This probably meant "large company" or "great band" of rebels, but was mistranslated in the late 19th century to refer to the "Great Society".
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317">
          <a href="#cite_note-317">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           302
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
      </ol>
     </div>
